  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_282fc649-b74e-4a6e-81ce-417a914161f1'
      });
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16785847919504453802"><b>A CULT MASTERPIECE—THE ADVENTURE NOVEL THAT INSPIRED JOHN HUSTON'S CLASSIC FILM, BY THE ELUSIVE AUTHOR WHO WAS A MODEL FOR THE HERO OF ROBERTO BOLAÑO'S <i>2666 </i></b><br/><b>
  <i></i>
</b><br/><b>

</b>Little is known for certain about B. Traven. Evidence suggests that he was born Otto Feige in Schlewsig-Holstein and that he escaped a death sentence for his involvement with the anarchist underground in Bavari</span>
  <span id="freeText16785847919504453802" style="display:none"><b>A CULT MASTERPIECE—THE ADVENTURE NOVEL THAT INSPIRED JOHN HUSTON'S CLASSIC FILM, BY THE ELUSIVE AUTHOR WHO WAS A MODEL FOR THE HERO OF ROBERTO BOLAÑO'S <i>2666 </i></b><br /><b>
  <i></i>
</b><br /><b>
  <i></i>
</b>Little is known for certain about B. Traven. Evidence suggests that he was born Otto Feige in Schlewsig-Holstein and that he escaped a death sentence for his involvement with the anarchist underground in Bavaria. Traven spent most of his adult life in Mexico, where, under various names, he wrote several bestsellers and was an outspoken defender of the rights of Mexico's indigenous people. First published in 1935, <i>The Treasure of the Sierra Madre </i>is Traven's most famous and enduring work, the dark, savagely ironic, and riveting story of three down-and-out Americans hunting for gold in Sonora.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16785847919504453802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_85759698" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85759698" />
    <a title="brian   " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/193310-brian"><img alt="brian   " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353195524p2/193310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/85759698?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="brian   " class="user" itemprop="url" name="brian   " href="/user/show/193310-brian">brian   </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6879605-the-treasure-of-the-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85759698" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9505594300028214376">Hal Croves, agent to novelist B. Traven, visited the set of his client's film <i>the treasure of the sierra madre</i>. croves had a german accent. he aroused the suspicion of many people, including director john huston. croves swiftly disappeared only to resurface in the 1950s in mexico city. upon his death in 1969 it was discovered that no record of birth for Hal Croves ever existed. <br/><br/><br/>Ret Marut was involved in leftist politics in germany in the 1920s. he was the editor of the radical magazine <i>der ziege</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9505594300028214376" style="display:none">Hal Croves, agent to novelist B. Traven, visited the set of his client's film <i>the treasure of the sierra madre</i>. croves had a german accent. he aroused the suspicion of many people, including director john huston. croves swiftly disappeared only to resurface in the 1950s in mexico city. upon his death in 1969 it was discovered that no record of birth for Hal Croves ever existed. <br><br><br>Ret Marut was involved in leftist politics in germany in the 1920s. he was the editor of the radical magazine <i>der ziegelbrenner</i>. in 1922 he was sentenced to death for his involvement in the bavarian soviet republic. he escaped to london. he spent time in prison in brixton. upon his release, he went to work as a fireman on a ship. he was never heard from again. copies of <i>der ziegelbrenner</i> were found in Hal Croves's archive. <br><br><br>Traven Torsvan first emerged in mexico in 1925. he was involved in a number of archaeological digs, showed much interest in the welfare of mexico's indian population, and ran an inn where he was known to the locals as <i>el gringo</i>. the mexican journalist Luis Spota discovered a bank account in the name of B. Traven, operated by Torsvan. when Spota suggested that Torsvan was B. Traven, Torsvan disappeared from history. <br><br><br>Otto Feige was the name given to police when they questioned Marut in london in 1923. in the 1970s Feige's brother was located and confirmed that a picture of Marut/Croves was his brother Otto who disappeared in 1905. their father worked in a factory that made coal briquettes. <i>der ziegelbrenner</i> is translated as 'the brickburner'. <br><br><br>B. Traven had been photographed once. his voice exists on one recording. he had a german accent.<br><br><br>other pseudonyms used by Croves/Marut/Torsvan/Traven: Albert Otto Max Wienecke, Fred Gaudet, Goetz Ohly, Anton Raderscheidt, Robert Bek-Gran, Hugo Kronthal, Wilhelm Scheider, Heinrich Otto Becker. <br><br><br> <br><br>if we follow the timeline from Feige to Marut to Torsvan to Croves, we find B. Traven drifting between the cracks like a literary phantom. we imagine Marut scribbling notes for novels between political rallies. or Torsvan, when all is silent and the inn's guests are asleep, plotting out the anti-capitalist pro-anarchist series of Traven's 'jungle' novels. we imagine Hal Croves having quite the laugh on the set of <i>sierra madre</i> as Croves, imaginary agent to B. Traven, the imaginary writer. <br><br>other theories claim that Marut/Croves/Torsvan/Traven was, in fact, the writer Jack London. some say he was Ambrose Bierce. others contend the true identity is Adolfo Mateos, former president of mexico. or that B. Traven is a name shared by many people, which could explain the anti-semitism of the early novels and the humanism of the later ones. others wonder why, if Croves lived until 1969, did Traven's output end by 1940. additionally: <i>the cotton pickers</i> was published in 1925, one year after Torsvan arrived in mexico -- could he, in that time, have absorbed such a rich sense of mexican culture and life?<br><br>Bolaño enthusiasts will notice something familiar: a german author who disappears and resurfaces throughout history, changing identities along the way, only to settle in mexico? B. Traven is, of course, Archimboldi. <br><br><br>the only known photograph of B. Traven: <br><br><span>[image error]</span> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9505594300028214376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16103398951855809690"><br/>Brilliant, radical, underrated, economically written in every sense of the word. I'm a big fan of the movie and I enjoyed the book even more, which is something I'm not necessarily always prone to do. <br/><br/>Powerfully captures the madness and paranoia inherent in the lust for riches, particularly when this takes place amid some hardscrabble vagabonds who don't really have much of a choice in the matter. <br/><br/>And the still-mysterious origins of the author (exiled German anarchist?   <span id="freeText16103398951855809690" style="display:none"><br />Brilliant, radical, underrated, economically written in every sense of the word. I'm a big fan of the movie and I enjoyed the book even more, which is something I'm not necessarily always prone to do. <br /><br />Powerfully captures the madness and paranoia inherent in the lust for riches, particularly when this takes place amid some hardscrabble vagabonds who don't really have much of a choice in the matter. <br /><br />And the still-mysterious origins of the author (exiled German anarchist? Mexican scribbler? Moonlighting union man?) only adds another fun layer of enigma (evidently he's the inspiration for Archimboldi in Bolano's 2666) to what is a very accessible, readable, finely-honed tale of bad men in hard times under a pitiless climate. <br /><br />"Anyone who is willing to work and is serious about it will certainly find a job. Only you must not go to the man who tells you this, for he has no job to offer and doesn't know anyone who knows of a vacancy. This is exactly the reason why he gives you such generous advice, out of brotherly love, and to demonstrate how little he knows the world."</span>

<div id="review_64088360" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64088360" />
    <a title="Rick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/764994-rick"><img alt="Rick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289746453p2/764994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64088360?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick" href="/user/show/764994-rick">Rick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/764994-rick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64088360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15501746000750876817">Traven’s classic novel, which became a classic movie, about greed, gold, and violence in the Mexican wilderness, is a thoughtful, entertaining morality tale. The movie followed Traven’s plot and dialogue pretty closely, except for some variation in the ending where the film collapses scenes for economy sake, and for a couple of long, but entertaining mining yarns told by Howard, the grizzled prospecting veteran. So if you have seen the movie, you know not only the plot and ending but you can see</span>
  <span id="freeText15501746000750876817" style="display:none">Traven’s classic novel, which became a classic movie, about greed, gold, and violence in the Mexican wilderness, is a thoughtful, entertaining morality tale. The movie followed Traven’s plot and dialogue pretty closely, except for some variation in the ending where the film collapses scenes for economy sake, and for a couple of long, but entertaining mining yarns told by Howard, the grizzled prospecting veteran. So if you have seen the movie, you know not only the plot and ending but you can see and hear the actors (Bogart, Tim Holt, the great Walter Huston, Alfonso Bedoya) performing the classic lines (“I don’t have to show you any stinking badges!”) <br /><br />B. Traven, whose biography and even his identity remains a bit of a mystery, has a strong, punchy prose style (Hemingway meets Hammett). He also has a partisan, though not naïve, view of those who thrive and fail along the frontiers of civilisation, where the very rich and powerful, usually by proxy, and the very poor and often desperate play a stacked game where the winners are the usual suspects and the losers everyone else. Howard is wise to the fact that there are two enemies, maybe three, but only one truly matters. The wealthy corporations, the Church, the bandits might get you but if you’re smart and careful you can beat them. The enemy that ultimately gets you is yourself, your own greed, it will turn you against your friends, your partners, yourself. That’s Howard’s warning to his partners Dobbs and Curtin and, when the trio find their fortune and only need to get to Durango safely with it, suspicion and madness emerges from greed’s fertile imagination. Traven has knowledge and affection for the Mexican landscape and people that is evidenced in his writing. Lovely descriptions of the mountains and deserts, of Mexican village life, bring the setting not just authenticity but, as they say, almost rise to the level of a critical character in the drama. Traven occasionally slips in anti-capitalist and anti-Church digs, given Mexico’s history and his sympathies, this is neither surprising nor inappropriate. They are generally brief asides and therefore not particularly disturbing to the tale he is telling. But the main story and almost all of the writing is focused, compelling and vigorous. John Huston got the movie right but B. Traven first got the novel right. The Treasure of Sierra Madre is a very good novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15501746000750876817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Checkman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Checkman" href="/user/show/5117003-checkman">Checkman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9780809437061" href="/book/show/13570581-the-treasure-of-the-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=my-fathers-books">my-fathers-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2441197025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2300573962455672823">The movie has been one of favorites for many years, but I never read the book. Until now that is. I have to say that it's a very readable book. Traven excels at conveying what life would have been like in Mexico back in the Twenties. He's very strong at atmosphere and the section of the novel when the men are working the gold mine is excellent. The book held my interest and kept me turning the pages. However it does have it's faults. <br/><br/>Much of the dialogue, and there is a significant amount of dia</span>
  <span id="freeText2300573962455672823" style="display:none">The movie has been one of favorites for many years, but I never read the book. Until now that is. I have to say that it's a very readable book. Traven excels at conveying what life would have been like in Mexico back in the Twenties. He's very strong at atmosphere and the section of the novel when the men are working the gold mine is excellent. The book held my interest and kept me turning the pages. However it does have it's faults. <br /><br />Much of the dialogue, and there is a significant amount of dialogue, is contrived. Perhaps clunky would be the better descriptor. However, if the mysterious Mr. Traven was actually a German then English would not have been his first language. No matter how skilled the translator converting one language to another always results in some artificiality and <i>The Treasure of the Sierra Madre</i> is not exempt from this phenomenon. <br /><br />Another issue with the novel is Traven's anti-clerical and anti-capitalist attitudes tend to crash in at times. Not that I necessarily disagree, but I don't need to be hit in the face with a board and there are times it is laid on pretty heavy. <br /><br />However , despite those two main quibbles (and a couple minor ones that I'm not going to go into at this time) the overall book is very good. It's an engrossing read and worth the time to dig into it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2300573962455672823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_657301143" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/657301143" />
    <a title="Ned" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21232850-ned"><img alt="Ned" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526900273p2/21232850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/657301143?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ned" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ned" href="/user/show/21232850-ned">Ned</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer657301143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17937267709793400550">Written in the 30's, this story provides intricate details about the adventures of the poor scrapping men trying to find work in Mexico. It is about Spanish and American imperialism, the power of capitalism, the despoiling of the environment (yet another oil boom), religious elitism and power mongering and (mostly) the omnipresent greed in the heart of every man. Three men seek their fortune in gold, finding that it is a chore and there are unimagined perils. An old man (grey in the beard) keeps</span>
  <span id="freeText17937267709793400550" style="display:none">Written in the 30's, this story provides intricate details about the adventures of the poor scrapping men trying to find work in Mexico. It is about Spanish and American imperialism, the power of capitalism, the despoiling of the environment (yet another oil boom), religious elitism and power mongering and (mostly) the omnipresent greed in the heart of every man. Three men seek their fortune in gold, finding that it is a chore and there are unimagined perils. An old man (grey in the beard) keeps them together, and spins tales and yarns aplenty and dispels wisdom that is largely ignored by the others. The writing is very masculine and quite humorous in the way the men dig at each other and use an unending assortment of nicknames for each other. The old man (Howard) seems to see into all their hearts and know what is coming. He assumes a zen-like presence, teaching the trade of prospecting and guiding them in their daily chores under the scorching heat and chilly nights. In the end there is much danger, and treachery and murder. But what mammon (gold in this case) creates in humans in universal, and morality weakens and greed wins in even the most strong. This is an anti-capitalistic book but highly entertaining and educational as we learn about the history of a time and place, native American and Spaniard domination, and the day to day habits of small town mountainous Mexico. The author seems to have had no editor, and the flawed grammar and syntax may be a translation problem, but it did not stymy my delight in the ribald dialogue and the . The author penetrated my vision with his crusty and flawed characters so easily recognizable by anyone who has mixed it up with those on the fringes of civilized life. In the end, the tragedy is complete as the object of the lads' efforts evaporates on the windswept plains and the cycle starts to repeat itself. OK, now I'm ready for the movie.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17937267709793400550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=lp1990s">lp1990s</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43030288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13936250493947731189">The author of this book, B. Traven, is a mystery man but his novels are some of the best moral adventure tales that I have ever read. <i>Treasure of the Sierra Madre</i> is his best known novel, probably due to the film version directed by John Huston and starring Humphrey Bogart. In it three Americans down on their luck prospect for gold in the Mexican Sierra Madre. A genuinely exciting adventure tale, it is also a psychological novel that takes us through the disintegration of one of the three, Dobbs</span>
  <span id="freeText13936250493947731189" style="display:none">The author of this book, B. Traven, is a mystery man but his novels are some of the best moral adventure tales that I have ever read. <i>Treasure of the Sierra Madre</i> is his best known novel, probably due to the film version directed by John Huston and starring Humphrey Bogart. In it three Americans down on their luck prospect for gold in the Mexican Sierra Madre. A genuinely exciting adventure tale, it is also a psychological novel that takes us through the disintegration of one of the three, Dobbs, as the gold they find corrupts his soul. The results provide for suspense and Traven's fine delineation of character makes the story both believable and interesting. Traven also explores the contrast of cultures with the white man's culture operating on the principle of greed while the Indians adhere to a myterious principle which they call "happiness". The difference between cultures is epitomized by the difficulty that Howard, the oldest member of the trio, has in explaining that for white men business is "happiness". The men in the novel are particular individuals but they are recognizable as universal types. The tone of the novel is serious but not without humor. with an ironic style Traven develops a well-rounded plot. The novel presents a powerful fable that is reminiscent of Chaucer's "Pardoner's Tale" in its lesson of "Radix malorum est Cupiditas" (greed is the root of all evil). <br />But most of all it is a story of outsiders, anarchic in its spirit, exciting in the adventure in spite of the tragic vision it portrays. I enjoyed both reading the novel and viewing the film adaptation. I highly recommend them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13936250493947731189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_520718268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/520718268" />
    <a title="Kathleen Fowler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3560723-kathleen-fowler"><img alt="Kathleen Fowler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288949581p2/3560723.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/520718268?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen Fowler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen Fowler" href="/user/show/3560723-kathleen-fowler">Kathleen Fowler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520718268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8571450780992210205">I’m very fond of the movie it inspired, so expected to enjoy this book. I did not. In my opinion the book is actually inferior to the film version. The writing is very uneven--some of it is downright atrocious. I’m not saying the book is without merit, but I approached it with expectations that were too high. <br/><br/>The story as it unfolds here is much darker than it was in the movie. The backdrop is Mexico, whose native population has been exploited for hundreds of years by the Catholic church, only t</span>
  <span id="freeText8571450780992210205" style="display:none">I’m very fond of the movie it inspired, so expected to enjoy this book. I did not. In my opinion the book is actually inferior to the film version. The writing is very uneven--some of it is downright atrocious. I’m not saying the book is without merit, but I approached it with expectations that were too high. <br /><br />The story as it unfolds here is much darker than it was in the movie. The backdrop is Mexico, whose native population has been exploited for hundreds of years by the Catholic church, only to fall now, in the 1920’s, under the sway of foreign oil companies who exploit the environment and the locals with equal abandon. Life is cheap, greed is rampant. <br /><br />The three men who form an uneasy alliance in order to make their fortunes in the Sierra Madre never let down their guard long enough to establish anything approaching real friendship. They have long become accustomed to believing that every man’s gain comes at the expense of another’s loss. Of the three, only the old man has any perspective. He is as eager to make his fortune as the others, but he also insists that the only real business of a man is “to live and be happy.” He respects the native peoples and the land. He shocks his partners by insisting that, before making off with their treasure, they must restore the mountain to the way it was when they found it. They grudgingly agree, and the time they spend at this task marks the closest they come to experiencing the joys of comradeship. <br /><br />This is a terribly depressing story. It’s a bleak look at how industrialization and materialism have eroded the natural world as well as the hearts of men. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8571450780992210205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106906728" />
    <a title="John Hood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1159765-john-hood"><img alt="John Hood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1210710102p2/1159765.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106906728?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Hood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Hood" href="/user/show/1159765-john-hood">John Hood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780809092970" href="/book/show/7105887-the-treasure-of-the-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106906728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13892662097209033068">Bound: SunPost Weekly June 3, 2010<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://bit.ly/8Y8g5x'>http://bit.ly/8Y8g5x</a><br/>When Badass Books Become Kickass Flicks<br/><br/>The Treasure of the Sierra Madre<br/>(Farrar Straus Giroux $16)<br/>At first glance this tale may seem less pulpy than the above, but its heart consists wholly of the stuff pulp lives are made of. To wit: losers still looking to win despite lives filled with nothing but loss – and not a damn chance in hell they’ll succeed.<br/><br/>If you’ve seen John Huston’s 1948 adaptation of B. Traven’s desolate 1927 classic, you’ll kno</span>
  <span id="freeText13892662097209033068" style="display:none">Bound: SunPost Weekly June 3, 2010<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bit.ly/8Y8g5x">http://bit.ly/8Y8g5x</a><br />When Badass Books Become Kickass Flicks<br /><br />The Treasure of the Sierra Madre<br />(Farrar Straus Giroux $16)<br />At first glance this tale may seem less pulpy than the above, but its heart consists wholly of the stuff pulp lives are made of. To wit: losers still looking to win despite lives filled with nothing but loss – and not a damn chance in hell they’ll succeed.<br /><br />If you’ve seen John Huston’s 1948 adaptation of B. Traven’s desolate 1927 classic, you’ll know it stars Humphrey Bogart and Tim Holt as two down-at-heels Americans basically living on the streets of Mexico, and Walter Huston as the fogy prospector who’s willing to partner-up in a quest for gold. You’ll also know that they come about as close as one can get without succeeding. Mostly though you’ll probably recall how the young men are driven to a madness that the old coot manages to keep at bay with a healthy dose of pragmatic fatalism.<br /><br />Scholars have said this is a retelling of Chaucer’s “The Pardoner’s Tale;” it might be more accurate to say it’s the American Dream exiled and then shot dead. Yes, visions of the good life dance in each of the principals’ heads, yet they’re undone by a paranoia that can only come from a deep distrust of their own damned selves. How can you have faith in a friend when you don’t even have faith in yourself? You can’t. And here the faithless are made to suffer their faults with a finality few could fathom, let alone endure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13892662097209033068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Traven writes fine characters, and while some of this novel went slowly, its purpose was to set up the ultimate punchline. I never saw the film, so I went into this cold. Brilliant novel. A good place to start if you’re new to B. Traven.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5857813733650915642">Three men go panning for gold in a world where there is no justice, only fortune.<br/><br/>This was a good but difficult read. It's very dry and inevitable, with each moment proceeding mechanically from the last.   <span id="freeText5857813733650915642" style="display:none">Three men go panning for gold in a world where there is no justice, only fortune.<br /><br />This was a good but difficult read. It's very dry and inevitable, with each moment proceeding mechanically from the last. If you've seen The Ballad of Buster Scruggs, that's what this feels like--the episode with Tom Waits, although I'm sure the reverse is true in terms of inspiration. Long. Drawn-out. Inevitable. And a bit mad.<br /><br />Good structure, with three tales-within-tales. The book feels a bit Canterbury Tales by the time you're done. I felt like this book could have been subtitled "An American Capitalistic Fairy Tale."<br /><br />Recommended if you like Westerns and Weird Westerns, especially Buster Scruggs or Brothers Sisters.</span>

<div id="review_1919508487" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1919508487" />
    <a title="Steve Carter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50812820-steve-carter"><img alt="Steve Carter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451482814p2/50812820.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1919508487?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve Carter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve Carter" href="/user/show/50812820-steve-carter">Steve Carter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1919508487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13278453896546916215">I love B Traven’s work, the 6 novels of the jungle series are unusual and terrific in their vivid illustration of the exploitation of labor in virtual slave conditions and the power in the human spirit to keep going and struggling. <br/>I finally got around to reading Treasure. It took me so long simply because it is the most well known. It is well know, at least the title, because of the 1948 Oscar winning film John Huston adaptation starring Humphry Bogart and Walter Huston. It’s a fine adventure m</span>
  <span id="freeText13278453896546916215" style="display:none">I love B Traven’s work, the 6 novels of the jungle series are unusual and terrific in their vivid illustration of the exploitation of labor in virtual slave conditions and the power in the human spirit to keep going and struggling. <br />I finally got around to reading Treasure. It took me so long simply because it is the most well known. It is well know, at least the title, because of the 1948 Oscar winning film John Huston adaptation starring Humphry Bogart and Walter Huston. It’s a fine adventure movie for sure. Of course I should have known that the novel would be so much more than this adventure movie which is essentially about greed and the lust for sand that can be exchanged for wealth.<br />Well, the novel is so much more. Even though it came earlier than the jungle series books it is pure Traven for sure. The mixture of boy adventure gold mining tale with Traven’s sort of Left anarchism social and political philosophy and admiration for the indigenous people of the region worked just fine for me. It is a wonderful book. <br />The action is interrupted a few times during the course of the story, as the characters tell tales of legionary previous mines. One of these stories that Howard tells before they begin their mining adventure goes on for 30 pages. <br />Huston’s Oscar winning script for the movie stayed fairly close to the plot of the novel with some obvious movieish simplification changes.<br />There is no other medium that can get inside the heads of the characters like a novel can. That is why I love to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13278453896546916215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer35802807" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11745626639043781730">I had kept hearing about how good of an author B. Traven was by many friends, and told of the mystery that surrounds the author - (no one knowing really who B. Traven is, or even if there is more than one author, what country the author originates from or anything else).<br/>And then a friend of mine gave me a copy of this book, and I devoured it. (Not literally)<br/>The story itself is exciting, a page turner, & like a Quentin Tarantino film - it has many layers of several different stories woven through</span>
  <span id="freeText11745626639043781730" style="display:none">I had kept hearing about how good of an author B. Traven was by many friends, and told of the mystery that surrounds the author - (no one knowing really who B. Traven is, or even if there is more than one author, what country the author originates from or anything else).<br />And then a friend of mine gave me a copy of this book, and I devoured it. (Not literally)<br />The story itself is exciting, a page turner, &amp; like a Quentin Tarantino film - it has many layers of several different stories woven throughout the novel.<br />His writing is so descriptive you would swear that this anonymous mysterious writer had lived the stories he writes about, it definitely drags you into the atmosphere of the book. <br />Most importantly is the way the author perfectly weaves good messages into the book, while not taking away from the story.<br />Right after finishing the novel I watched the film, which - being a good film, it still pales in comparison to the book, not to mention that the film omits a lot from the book, and changes the story around a lot.<br />After reading "The Treasure of the Sierra Madre", I bypassed all the books I've had on my to-read list (which I never do!) and went straight for a borrowed copy of his book "The Death Ship" which so far, is turning out to be another classic.<br /><br /> </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/7297793-david">David</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2859077712&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2859077712" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9368685294614603088">When I read this years ago, I put it in the category of novels that were actually improved in the film version (I list Elmer Gantry in that group). I thought it wandered (literarally) too much, and could use a good editor. And the frequent narrator comments on capitalism (although much of it warranted) bordered on propaganda. In this reading, I warmed up to it. Although I still think it wandered too much, I realized that he was haranguing against human nature, not just capitalism, and had a noir</span>
  <span id="freeText9368685294614603088" style="display:none">When I read this years ago, I put it in the category of novels that were actually improved in the film version (I list Elmer Gantry in that group). I thought it wandered (literarally) too much, and could use a good editor. And the frequent narrator comments on capitalism (although much of it warranted) bordered on propaganda. In this reading, I warmed up to it. Although I still think it wandered too much, I realized that he was haranguing against human nature, not just capitalism, and had a noir-like acceptance of that nature. And the film did follow the novel pretty closely. The one major difference in the movie was the death of Cody, and the final impression that Curtin would seek out his widow to tell her of his death. It added a human warmth that further complicated, and so enriched, that complex human nature that permeates the novel.</span>

<div id="review_1083233404" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1083233404" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1083233404?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruthie Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruthie Jones" href="/user/show/1119571-ruthie-jones">Ruthie Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9780809437061" href="/book/show/13570581-the-treasure-of-the-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1119571-ruthie-jones?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1083233404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16643795922318700653">I find this story (both the book and the movie) truly horrifying in that it explores the depths of the human mind in terms of greed and conscience. These traits are expressed explicitly through Dobbs. The horror really begins torward the end with the chapter that starts with "A night of horror began for Curtin. Not so for Dobbs." Scary!<br/><br/>Dobbs clearly goes off the deep end and pays a heavy price. Juxtaposed with the relatively loose honesty of Curtin and Howard, Dobbs's break with sanity is that m</span>
  <span id="freeText16643795922318700653" style="display:none">I find this story (both the book and the movie) truly horrifying in that it explores the depths of the human mind in terms of greed and conscience. These traits are expressed explicitly through Dobbs. The horror really begins torward the end with the chapter that starts with "A night of horror began for Curtin. Not so for Dobbs." Scary!<br /><br />Dobbs clearly goes off the deep end and pays a heavy price. Juxtaposed with the relatively loose honesty of Curtin and Howard, Dobbs's break with sanity is that much more pronounced and frightening. Why? Because we wonder who we would end up becoming in a similar situation.<br /><br />While not listed as horror or mystery or just plain scary, The Treasure of the Sierra Madre gives us a look into the human condition in all its terrifying glory. The human mind is one of the scariest places on earth.<br /><br />***<br />"It is always more convenient to dream of what might be."<br /><br />". . .gold is a very devilish sort of thing, believe me, boys. In the first place, it changes your character entirely. When you have it your soul is no longer the same as it was before."<br /><br />"Tell you, the best thing is going alone. Of course you must have the guts to stand loneliness. Lots of guys go nutty being alone for a long time. On the other hand, going with a partner or two is dangerous. All the time murder's lurking about."<br /><br />"It isn't gold that changes man, it is the power which gold gives to man that changes the soul of man. This power, though, is only imaginary. If not recognizable by other men, it does not exist."<br /><br />"If you wish to survive, you have to win the battle."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16643795922318700653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1508931815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1508931815" />
    <a title="Paulina Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4428808-paulina-sanchez"><img alt="Paulina Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1414389324p2/4428808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1508931815?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paulina Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paulina Sanchez" href="/user/show/4428808-paulina-sanchez">Paulina Sanchez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789682927416" href="/book/show/26092861-el-tesoro-de-la-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Paulina by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/48863633-ulises">Ulises</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4428808-paulina-sanchez?shelf=books-in-spanish">books-in-spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1508931815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10569005126510821504">This is my first novel by Traven, before I had only read his short stories and loved them. <br/><br/>Tesoro is an epic story, it is a story about the mountains, about gold, about greed and what it does to men and women who go in search of riches and fortune. Not only that, it talks about destiny, how sometimes these things fall on your lap, like a test from life itself. Do you buy that lottery ticket? What if you don't win? What if you do? It is about keeping one's humanity when one could easily lose it f</span>
  <span id="freeText10569005126510821504" style="display:none">This is my first novel by Traven, before I had only read his short stories and loved them. <br /><br />Tesoro is an epic story, it is a story about the mountains, about gold, about greed and what it does to men and women who go in search of riches and fortune. Not only that, it talks about destiny, how sometimes these things fall on your lap, like a test from life itself. Do you buy that lottery ticket? What if you don't win? What if you do? It is about keeping one's humanity when one could easily lose it for "worthless" pieces of gold. It is about recognising and cherishing the worth of people and culture, rather than social status. <br /><br />I loved how Traven tells not just one story of three men and their search for gold in the Sierra Madre, he also tells stories of others who have searched (and found) treasure and what happened to them. I liked the stories having to do with the natives and their encounter with modern society. Not only that but also hearing the natives' perspective on the white men and their madness when it comes to gold and oil. <br /><br />It's a novel packed with social criticism that's still relevant today, about cultures that are in danger of extinction, and about human nature corrupted by things that should remain in the ground. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10569005126510821504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1836286064" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1836286064" />
    <a title="Williwaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3491750-williwaw"><img alt="Williwaw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304797883p2/3491750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1836286064?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Williwaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Williwaw" href="/user/show/3491750-williwaw">Williwaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/33281758-the-treasure-of-the-sierra-madre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1836286064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16965960801313742230">The portrait of Humphrey Bogart on the cover of this book has been staring me down for at least a couple years now. I must have bought this book after re-watching the fantastic John Huston film adaptation. The film has once again faded from memory, so I am now, of course, eager to see it once again.<br/><br/>Traven's novel is a well-told adventure story, set in Mexico, with themes of greed and duplicity. I can see why it may have been a best-seller in the U.S., back in the 1930's, as the country was recov</span>
  <span id="freeText16965960801313742230" style="display:none">The portrait of Humphrey Bogart on the cover of this book has been staring me down for at least a couple years now. I must have bought this book after re-watching the fantastic John Huston film adaptation. The film has once again faded from memory, so I am now, of course, eager to see it once again.<br /><br />Traven's novel is a well-told adventure story, set in Mexico, with themes of greed and duplicity. I can see why it may have been a best-seller in the U.S., back in the 1930's, as the country was recovering from the Great Depression. The writing is accessible and fairly pedestrian, with some occasional touches of artistry. Traven's intimacy with Mexico is evident, and paints a convincing portrait of the country. In the manner of an epic, the novel plays host to several "frame" tales (long, fabulous narratives told by the immediate characters). <br /><br />Just over 300 pages in my edition, this is a slow, sweeping tale that was just good enough to drag this reader along for the ride. John Huston's 1948 film version is undoubtedly the superior work (being one of the greatest films of all time, IMHO), so if one must choose between the two, the choice is obvious. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16965960801313742230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1158725847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17137872268566767563">The story was interesting enough to keep me hooked despite the dense language. Every so often little gems of lines would pop through making me think David Sedaris had clearly written this story has an homage to westerns and that all the tales of this book being old were inside jokes I never got. But then, blatant racism would draw me back to the time when authors could only say 'funking' as not to be censored. These tid bits still leave puzzled and question the well written morals of this story.</span>
  <span id="freeText17137872268566767563" style="display:none">The story was interesting enough to keep me hooked despite the dense language. Every so often little gems of lines would pop through making me think David Sedaris had clearly written this story has an homage to westerns and that all the tales of this book being old were inside jokes I never got. But then, blatant racism would draw me back to the time when authors could only say 'funking' as not to be censored. These tid bits still leave puzzled and question the well written morals of this story. Nonetheless, a must read.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer664888450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3567909555666630156">This book is an underrated masterpiece. It's the basis for the classic John Huston movie of the same name ("We don't need no badges! I don't have to show you any stinkin' badges!"). Nobody seems to know who or what a "B. Traven" is, but he produced a compelling and beautifully written novel. As one of the Mexican bandits in the story might say, read it you goddamned cabrón and chinga tu madre!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2509763286" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2509763286" />
    <a title="Malcolm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3340373-malcolm"><img alt="Malcolm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267085906p2/3340373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2509763286?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malcolm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malcolm" href="/user/show/3340373-malcolm">Malcolm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3340373-malcolm?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3340373-malcolm?shelf=hutt-u3a-book-group-2018">hutt-u3a-book-group-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2509763286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8155549260372620440">The Treasure of the Sierra Madre is often referred to as a classic but it is not easy to find a copy. I could not find it in print and Project Gutenberg does not list it, presumably because no one is certain if the book is out of copyright; the author B. Traven, a recluse, presumably died about 1967.<br/>In fact, the story of B. Traven is more interesting than his novel. We can say he was active in the 1920s to the 1940s, wrote in German but lived in Mexico and that B. Traven is an alias.<br/>The early pa</span>
  <span id="freeText8155549260372620440" style="display:none">The Treasure of the Sierra Madre is often referred to as a classic but it is not easy to find a copy. I could not find it in print and Project Gutenberg does not list it, presumably because no one is certain if the book is out of copyright; the author B. Traven, a recluse, presumably died about 1967.<br />In fact, the story of B. Traven is more interesting than his novel. We can say he was active in the 1920s to the 1940s, wrote in German but lived in Mexico and that B. Traven is an alias.<br />The early parts of the novel remind me of writers like Theodore Dreiser and John Steinbeck with the depressing account of Dobbs, an American looking for work in an unnamed port in Mexico. The town is in an oil boom, but Dobbs can find little work and when he does, he is cheated of his wages. While this part of the novel seems unnecessarily long it does provide a motivation for why Dobbs and his fellow American, Curtin, go along with Howard's scheme to find a lost gold mine in the Sierra Madre mountains, using a dubious looking map which Howard has found.<br />Once the hunt for the gold begins, the reader can have more confidence in Traven. His description of life in the isolated mountain peaks has the ring of truth in contrast to his description of the jungle where we are asked to believe that Mexico has lions and tigers!!<br />As the three men find and accumulate alluvial gold we see Traven's skill in developing character. Howard presents as a decent person although his knowledge of how to navigate in the desert, build equipment to pan for gold and bring native boys back to life seem a little too much. The portrayal of Curtin however, is very well done. His deterioration into greed, suspicion and madness is both believable and chilling.<br />The final chapters of the novel however become quite confusing. Curtin shoots Dobbs but Howard returns in time to restore Dobbs to health. The Mounted Police drive off the Gold Hat gang of bandits who are still able to return to kill and rob Curtin but fail to see that he is hiding a fortune in gold. Then the police return and retrieve Howard and Dobbs' possessions including the gold.<br />The sub plots, that break into the main action, are a distraction. I'm not sure we need a story about how the lost gold mine became lost as the three men never find the mine and instead make their fortunes panning for alluvial gold. Nor do we need the history of the Gold Hat Gang, their existence can be accepted without an explanation.<br />Much of this novel is amateurish but the middle section showing how greed can destroy people makes it worthwhile reading the novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8155549260372620440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="María Vargas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26492620-mar-a-vargas"><img alt="María Vargas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587791477p2/26492620.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2863173502?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="María Vargas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="María Vargas" href="/user/show/26492620-mar-a-vargas">María Vargas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26492620-mar-a-vargas?shelf=hidden-treasures">hidden-treasures</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2863173502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1156552098805407158">B. Traven... oh, this author... is definitely one of my favorites, and one I never thought I'd run into in the most unexpected possible way. <br/><br/>A year ago, walking through the center of my city, I decided to enter one of my favorite old bookstores in Germany. I was prowling around every corner, taking out books and putting them away, walking as slowly as possible through all those piles of books so that I wouldn't miss anything important. Then, amidst these piles and rows of books everywhere, I stu</span>
  <span id="freeText1156552098805407158" style="display:none">B. Traven... oh, this author... is definitely one of my favorites, and one I never thought I'd run into in the most unexpected possible way. <br /><br />A year ago, walking through the center of my city, I decided to enter one of my favorite old bookstores in Germany. I was prowling around every corner, taking out books and putting them away, walking as slowly as possible through all those piles of books so that I wouldn't miss anything important. Then, amidst these piles and rows of books everywhere, I stumbled upon a small yellow book of pasta. It looked quite old, and mysterious.<br />I decided to open it and read a few pages, and it was enough for me to read less than five to know that this was a treasure, and I had to take it now or now!<br />I'm even somehow amazed by the fact that being in german the book wasn't absolutely hard to read! <br /><br />I bought it together with one by Göethe and although it was such a short book, I fell completely in love with B. Traven's stories. So fresh, so realistic, so raw, so profound, so worldly comical about the ironies of life. I would read them over and over again, without getting tired.<br /><br />This is my second book by B. Traven, and although I only have two, it is enough for me to be overshadowed by every story I read by him.<br /><br />Besides, the mystery that surrounds the author regarding his true identity and his refuge in the beautiful Mexican lands makes it all seem even more magical and worth appreciating and knowing. These books are definitely treasures to me. So, I just hope someday I can go back into an old bookstore like this and find another hidden treasure from B. Traven. A relic.</span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2748380589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10411836711750577665">This meditation on all natures of work focuses on a louche American wayfarer, Dobbs, whom we meet begging on the streets of a Mexican oil town.<br/><br/>What's it going to take to get this guy to work? First comes a buddy, with whom he sets off on a road trip to find work at inland wells, then comes a prospector, whose tales of mountain gold mines set them off for the titular mountain range.<br/><br/>Traven connects scenes of adventure with mining tales told by Howard, the senior of the three miners. These tales he</span>
  <span id="freeText10411836711750577665" style="display:none">This meditation on all natures of work focuses on a louche American wayfarer, Dobbs, whom we meet begging on the streets of a Mexican oil town.<br /><br />What's it going to take to get this guy to work? First comes a buddy, with whom he sets off on a road trip to find work at inland wells, then comes a prospector, whose tales of mountain gold mines set them off for the titular mountain range.<br /><br />Traven connects scenes of adventure with mining tales told by Howard, the senior of the three miners. These tales help to spice up somewhat boring parts of the plot (day after day, digging for gold), and to foreshadow challenges with bandits, transportation, and thirst.<br /><br />These tales are, of course, missing from the film, but the film does a better job of cutting out Dobbs's early efforts to find work, and it explains the presence--and the end--of Lacaud--in a much better way. <br /><br />To me, this is a good adventure tale, definitely worth the read, but probably nowhere near the status of "classic" of the genre that motivated me to buy and read it.</span>

<div id="review_2337135447" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2337135447" />
    <a title="Debbie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2134607-debbie"><img alt="Debbie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1366234276p2/2134607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2337135447?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie" href="/user/show/2134607-debbie">Debbie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2337135447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11658631503610477773">First off, yes the book does have the stinking badges line. Traven's societal concerns are a little too in the forefront for my taste. He's not a fan of the Catholic church, the boss man, or, sometimes the government. The main characters, Americans in Mexico in the 1920s, don't have much of a past. We don't know why they are there, if they are running from the law, just restless, or anything else. Perhaps this is reflective of the author's own disputed and mysterious past. The pursuit of gold co</span>
  <span id="freeText11658631503610477773" style="display:none">First off, yes the book does have the stinking badges line. Traven's societal concerns are a little too in the forefront for my taste. He's not a fan of the Catholic church, the boss man, or, sometimes the government. The main characters, Americans in Mexico in the 1920s, don't have much of a past. We don't know why they are there, if they are running from the law, just restless, or anything else. Perhaps this is reflective of the author's own disputed and mysterious past. The pursuit of gold corrupts all those in pursuit of it, except perhaps, the old wise prospector Howard. Otherwise, those with power and wealth use it against those who don't; and make more off of the back of the laborers, who are easy to take advantage of since they have no power. There is a feel for the varied landscape and a rather surprising interest in ecology. Worth reading if you aren't expecting a real adventure story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11658631503610477773" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Traven<br />😀📚<br />There are too many things to love about this timeless classic book written in 1935.<br /><br />The story of three American adventurers who hunt for gold in the mountains of Mexico.<br /><br />Source of memorable quotes such as this one: ***It isn't the gold that changes man, it is the power which gold gives to man that changes the soul of man. Traven, remains anonymous to this day, however it is rumored that he posed as his own literary agent, Hal Croves, on the set of the John Huston film<br />📚📚📚📚📚</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qL5hVYQnqtWMrYcjhoT0SOK1CxXmHbTcgT9ah2vzprZ2w5AV6Me2wPaE5rAI5/ctQfnyapjFk2rXkLf9DVoMwQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_51243325'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51243325-wilmington-s-lie?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Wilmington&#39;s Lie: The Murderous Coup of 1898 and the Rise of White Supremacy<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/31200.David_Zucchino?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">David Zucchino<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.39 avg rating &mdash; 757 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2020\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6941295460180896784\">From Pulitzer Prize winner, David Zucchino, comes a searing account of the Wilmington riot and coup of 1898, an extraordinary event unknown to most Americans.\n\nBy the 1890s, Wilmington was North Carolina\'s largest city and a shining example of a mixe<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6941295460180896784\" style=\"display:none\">From Pulitzer Prize winner, David Zucchino, comes a searing account of the Wilmington riot and coup of 1898, an extraordinary event unknown to most Americans.\n\nBy the 1890s, Wilmington was North Carolina\'s largest city and a shining example of a mixed-race community. It was a bustling port city with a burgeoning African American middle class and a Fusionist government of Republicans and Populists that included black aldermen, police officers and magistrates. There were successful black-owned businesses and an African American newspaper, The Record. But across the state--and the South--white supremacist Democrats were working to reverse the advances made by former slaves and their progeny.\n\nIn 1898, in response to a speech calling for white men to rise to the defense of Southern womanhood against the supposed threat of black predators, Alexander Manly, the outspoken young Record editor, wrote that some relationships between black men and white women were consensual. His editorial ignited outrage across the South, with calls to lynch Manly.\n\nBut North Carolina\'s white supremacist Democrats had a different strategy. They were plotting to take back the state legislature in November &quot;by the ballot or bullet or both,&quot; and then use the Manly editorial to trigger a &quot;race riot&quot; to overthrow Wilmington\'s multi-racial government. Led by prominent citizens including Josephus Daniels, publisher of the state\'s largest newspaper, and former Confederate Colonel Alfred Moore Waddell, white supremacists rolled out a carefully orchestrated campaign that included raucous rallies, race-baiting editorials and newspaper cartoons, and sensational, fabricated news stories.\n\nWith intimidation and violence, the Democrats suppressed the black vote and stuffed ballot boxes (or threw them out), to win control of the state legislature on November eighth. Two days later, more than 2,000 heavily armed Red Shirts swarmed through Wilmington, torching the Record office, terrorizing women and children, and shooting at least sixty black men dead in the streets. The rioters forced city officials to resign at gunpoint and replaced them with mob leaders. Prominent blacks--and sympathetic whites--were banished. Hundreds of terrified black families took refuge in surrounding swamps and forests.\n\nThis brutal insurrection is a rare instance of a violent overthrow of an elected government in the U.S. It halted gains made by blacks and restored racism as official government policy, cementing white rule for another half century. It was not a &quot;race riot,&quot; as the events of November 1898 came to be known, but rather a racially motivated rebellion launched by white supremacists.\n\nIn Wilmington\'s Lie, Zucchino uses contemporary newspaper accounts, diaries, letters and official communications to create a gripping and compelling narrative that weaves together individual stories of hate and fear and brutality. This is a dramatic and definitive account of a remarkable but forgotten chapter of American history.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6941295460180896784\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'8_book_51243325\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp wtrLeft\'>\n<form action=\"/shelf/add_to_shelf\" The drug cart<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3788619981307648339\" style=\"display:none\">Veering between carnival and apocalypse, Mexico has in the last ten years become the epicentre of the international drug trade. The so-called &quot;war on drugs&quot; has been a brutal and chaotic failure (more than 160,000 lives have been lost). The drug cartels and the forces of law and order are often in collusion, corruption is everywhere. Life is cheap and inconvenient people - the poor, the unlucky, the honest or the inquisitive - can be &quot;disappeared&quot; leaving not a trace behind (in September 2015, more than 26,798 were officially registered as &quot;not located&quot;). Yet people in all walks of life have refused to give up.\n\nDiego Enrique Osorno and Juan Villoro tell stories of teenage prostitution and Mexico\'s street children. Anabel Hernández and Emiliano Ruiz Parra give chilling accounts of the &quot;disappearance&quot; of forty-three students and the murder of a self-educated land lawyer. Sergio González Rodríguez and Marcela Turati dissect the impact of the violence on the victims and those left behind, while Lydia Cacho contributes a journal of what it is like to live every day of your life under threat of death. Reading these accounts we begin to understand the true nature of the meltdown of democracy, obscured by lurid headlines, and the sheer physical \nand intellectual courage needed to oppose it.\n\nThis is reportage at its bravest and most necessary - writing that has the power to change the world\'s view of Mexico and by the force of its truth to start to heal the country\'s many sorrows.<\/span>\n
